Finance Director.
Taxed Activities. For the privilege of engaging in business in the State of New Mexico, the Gross
Receipts Tax is imposed upon any person engaging in business in the State. “Gross Receipts” is defined
in the Gross Receipts and Compensating Tax Act as the total amount of money or value or other
consideration received from selling property in the State of New Mexico (including tangible personal
property handled on consignment in the State), from leasing property employed in the State of New
Mexico, from performing services in the State of New Mexico and from selling services outside New21
Mexico, the product of which is initially used in New Mexico. The definition excludes cash discounts
allowed and taken, the Gross Receipts Tax payable on transactions for the reporting period and any
county sales tax, county fire protection excise tax, county and municipal gross receipts taxes, any time or
time-price differential and certain gross receipts or sales taxes imposed by an Indian tribe or pueblo.
Unlike most other states, the State taxes sales of services, including legal services and certain medical
services.
Exemptions and Deductions. Some activities and industries are exempt from the Gross Receipts
Tax by virtue of their taxation under other laws. Exemptions include but are not limited to receipts of
governmental agencies and certain organizations (some of which are taxable by the State, with no local
distribution), receipts from the sale of vehicles, occasional sales of property or services, wages, certain
agricultural products, dividends, receipts from the sale of prescription drugs and certain federal
government paid medical expenses and interest and receipts from the sale of or leasing of natural gas, oil
or mineral interests. Various deductions are also allowed, including, but not limited to, receipts from the
sale or lease of real property, receipts from various types of sales or leases of tangible personal property
or services, receipts from sales to governmental agencies or certain organizations, receipts from
processing certain agricultural products, receipts from certain publication sales, certain receipts from
interstate commerce transactions and receipts from the sale of certain food and certain medical services.
In spite of the numerous specified exemptions and deductions from gross receipts taxation, the general
presumption is that all receipts of a person engaging in business in the State and the City are subject to
gross receipts tax.
Any municipal ordinance imposing an increment of municipal gross receipts tax such as the
Environmental Services Gross Receipts Tax is required to adopt by reference the same definitions and the
same provisions relating to exemptions and deductions as are established for the State gross receipts tax.
In addition, the Environmental Services Gross Receipts Tax may not be imposed on gross receipts arising
from (1) the transmission of messages by wire or other means from one point within the municipality to
another point outside the municipality; (2) transporting persons or property for hire by railroad, motor
vehicle, air transportation or any other means from one point within the municipality to another point
outside the municipality; or (3) a business located outside the boundaries of a municipality on land owned
by that municipality for which a gross receipts tax distribution is made pursuant to the Gross Receipts and
Compensating Tax Act.
Legislative Changes. Revisions to laws of the State affecting taxed activities and distributions of
gross receipts tax revenues could be adopted in the future by the State Legislature. Proposals affecting
taxed activities and distributions are frequently considered by the State Legislature. There is no assurance
that any future revisions to State laws will not adversely affect activities now subject to gross receipts tax
or distribution of gross receipts tax revenues to the City. See “SPECIAL FACTORS RELATING TO
THE BONDS – Legislative Changes” herein.
Manner of Collection of Environmental Services Gross Receipt Taxes . Businesses must make
their payments of gross receipts taxes on or before the twenty-fifth of each month for taxable events in the
prior month. Environmental Services Gross Receipts Taxes are collected by the Revenue Division in the
same manner and at the same time that the Revenue Division collects the other gross receipts tax revenues
imposed by the State and the City. The Revenue Division remits to the City the revenues collected from
the Environmental Services Gross Receipts Tax less any deduction for administrative cost and less any
disbursements for tax credits, refunds and the payment of interest applicable to such tax. Distribution of
the revenues from the Environmental Services Gross Receipts Tax is made within the month following
the month in which the taxes are collected.22
Remedies for Delinquent Taxes . The Revenue Division may assess gross receipts taxes to a
taxpayer who has not paid the taxes due to the State. If any taxpayer to whom gross receipts taxes have
been assessed or upon whom demand for payment has been made does not make payment thereof (or
protest the assessment or demand for payment) within 30 days after the date of assessment or demand for
payment, the taxpayer becomes a delinquent taxpayer. Such taxpayer remains delinquent until payment
of all the taxes due, including interest and penalties, or until security is furnished for the payment thereof.
The Revenue Division may, under certain circumstances, enter into an agreement with a delinquent
taxpayer to permit monthly installment payments for a period of not more than 36 months. Interest is due
on any delinquent tax from the first day following the day on which it is due at the rate of 1.25% per
month until paid, without regard to any installment agreement. However, if the Gross Receipts Tax is
paid within 10 days after demand is made, no interest shall be imposed for the period after the date of
demand.
The Revenue Division may levy upon all property or rights to property of a delinquent taxpayer
and sell the same in order to collect the delinquent tax. The amount of delinquent Gross Receipts Taxes is
also a lien in favor of the State upon all property and rights to property of the delinquent taxpayer, which
lien may be foreclosed as provided by State statutes.

THE WASTEWATER UTILITY SYSTEM
Ownership of Wastewater Utility System
The City has owned and operated the Wastewater Utility System since the inception of
wastewater collection and treatment within the corporate limits of the City. Among the first collection
lines installed were those to serve the state capitol district. As the corporate limits of the City have
grown, both the type and location of its wastewater treatment facilities have changed. The City’s
treatment facilities are currently located at 73 Paseo Real, which is southwest of downtown Santa Fe and
north of the Santa Fe Municipal Airport.
Operation of the collection and treatment portions of the Wastewater Utility System is the
responsibility of the Wastewater Management Division of the Public Utilities Department of the City.
The Public Utilities Department manages three enterprise utilities owned by the City: Environmental
Services, Wastewater Management and Water Services. The Wastewater Management Division’s
mission is to ensure that all sewage produced within the service area is collected, conveyed and treated in
compliance with local, state and federal regulations and guidelines, and in so doing, to protect the public’s
health and welfare.
The Wastewater Management Division is comprised of seven sections having a total of
67 employees.
Section
Number of
Employees
Administration 5
Treatment 24
Collection 21
Engineering and Utility Development 5
Industrial Pre-Treatment 4
Water Quality Laboratory 3
Composting 5
Total 67
Personnel engaged in the operation of the treatment plant and in maintenance of the collection
system are required by the United States Environmental Protection Agency (“EPA”) and State of New
Mexico Environment Department (the “State Environmental Department”) regulations to maintain
state-sanctioned certification.
Customer Characteristics
During the fiscal year ended June 30, 2018, the Wastewater Utility System had approximately
37,822 City and County customers. Of this number, 33,450 were residential and 4,372 were commercial
customers. The table below summarizes certain characteristics of the customers served by the
Wastewater Utility System over the last five fiscal years (July 1 to June 30). Since the City has a unitary
wastewater rate structure that relates to quantity of water flow, the distinction between commercial and
residential customers is essentially one of nature of use. That is, commercial customers are designated on
the basis of variable water use related to business applications and not just to total quantity or to
classification for other City utility (i.e., water or refuse) rates. Hence, an apartment house, state office
building or school is classified as residential since water use varies during the course of a year with
seasonal exterior use and occupancy and not due to variable business activity. By contrast, businesses27
such as laundries, hotels, service stations, car washes, and restaurants have highly variable water use
directly related to level of business and are treated as commercial.

Description of Existing Facilities
The Wastewater Utility System operates in compliance with the National Pollution Discharge
Elimination System (“NPDES”) permit NM0022292 issued by the EPA, State Environmental Department28
Discharge permit DP-289 and a sludge disposal permit DP-135. The Wastewater Utility System consists
of three major components, treatment, collection and general buildings and facilities.
Treatment. The City operates a single 13-million gallon per day (“mgd”) tertiary treatment
facility (the “Wastewater Treatment Facility”) located at 73 Paseo Real. Treatment involves nutrient
removal through aeration and activated sludge. Filtration and ultraviolet light disinfection were instituted
in early 1998. Treated sludge is currently composted and reused.
In calendar year 2017, the peak influent flow (peak day) at the Wastewater Treatment Facility
was 7.781 mgd, the low flow (minimum day) was 4.367 mgd, and the average daily flow was 5.169 mgd.
Treated effluent is discharged to the Santa Fe River. In calendar year 2018, the peak influent flow at the
Wastewater Treatment Facility was 7.25 mgd, the low flow (minimum day) was 3.68 mgd, and the
average daily flow was 5.14 mgd.
The treatment operation includes a laboratory which performs continuous process testing and
analyses. The laboratory also provides the chemical and biological analyses required for the City’s
periodic compliance reporting required as a condition of its NPDES permit including sludge and effluent
quality. Septage delivered for treatment is also tested.
Santa Fe is not an industrialized community. The City’s economic base is heavily weighted to
government, tourism, regional business and retail services. Wastewater is largely domestic in nature.
However, the City has adopted an EPA-approved Industrial Pretreatment (“IPT”) program which requires
the issuance of permits to certain classes of users as required by federal regulation. Presently the City has
issued and has outstanding four such permits. The City enforces a local fee system adopted by ordinance
for users who discharge wastewater whose constituents present a treatment burden. See “—User Rates
and Billing.” The City also receives for treatment septic wastes delivered to a receiving station located at
the Wastewater Treatment Facility. The service area is regional as there is no other wastewater treatment
plant which receives such waste in northern New Mexico.
The following table summarizes the total amount of influent treated at the Wastewater Treatment
Facility for the last five calendar years.
Year

Source: City of Santa Fe, Public Utilities Department
Collection. The City operates and maintains approximately 365 miles of sanitary sewer
collection lines which collect and transport wastewater to the Wastewater Treatment Facility. There are
no combined sanitary and storm sewer lines. Sanitary sewer lines range in size from six inches to
30 inches. The collection system is continuously evaluated. The system is divided into maintenance29
areas, based upon age and type of pipe material. Maintenance is performed and conditional assessments
are made utilizing a NASSCO rating system. The division utilizes this information to develop a
rehabilitation plan annually and to undertake rehabilitation projects.
Flow to the Wastewater Treatment Facility in a vast portion of the Wastewater Utility System is
by means of gravity. As a matter of policy the City has avoided the use of public lift stations. Because of
very localized topographic constraints, the City operates three lift station which can serve approximately
600 residences. The City presently encourages the use of individual grinder pumps for lots which by
virtue of site specific circumstances cannot make a gravity connection.
There are six crews engaged in maintaining sewer lines and responding to emergencies in the
system. The majority of the wastewater operation’s motorized equipment is involved in this function.
General Buildings and Facilities . The Wastewater Management Division operates from the
same 54-acre site on which the present Wastewater Treatment Facility is located. The Wastewater
Treatment Facility was opened in 1984 and replaced a wastewater treatment plant that had been in
operation since the early 1960s. Several outbuildings related to the previous plant are still being used. In
constructing the Wastewater Treatment Facility, the City consolidated its wastewater treatment functions
at the new location and closed a second treatment plant which was located on Siler Road within the
corporate limits of the City.
In addition to the equipment that controls and monitors the Wastewater Treatment Facility
functions, the main treatment building also contains offices for division management staff. Due to growth
in responsibilities and staffing, the laboratory was moved out of this main building to a new separate
structure in 1996. A former residence for the plant superintendent which had been erected as part of the
original 1963 plant has been converted into additional office space for the plant maintenance staff. Due to
increased staffing engaged in general utility development, engineering functions, environmental issues
and the IPT program, a new building was constructed in 2001. A new training conference room was
created in an area that housed the administration building of the 1963 plant. A single building dating
from 1991 is used by those engaged in maintenance of the collection system. A new metal building was
constructed in 2003, which is used as a workshop and to store the large tools and equipment. The
warehouse was moved to one central location (two large garage bays) and a small auxiliary office
building was built in 2009 for the warehouse staff. A compost and dewatering facility was added (built) to
the facility in 2008.
In 1963, when the original treatment facility was put into service, the site was located outside the
City’s municipal water service area. A domestic well was used to provide for all potable water needs at
this location. In 1998, the Water Division of the City designed and built an extension from the municipal
water lines to the Wastewater Treatment Facility to provide domestic water and fire protection service.
User Rates and Billing
Rates are evaluated at least once annually in relation to projected operations and maintenance
expenditures as part of the City’s budget formulation process. Rates are adopted as a matter of ordinance
and are codified. Changes to rates are adopted through an amendment of ordinance. There are two
customer classes, residential and commercial.
The City bills monthly for sewer service. Bills consist of fixed and variable charges. The
monthly fixed portion of a bill is the same amount for each billed account ($7.18 per month per account).
The monthly residential variable charge is $4.42 per 1,000 gallons of total water use metered for the
months of December, January and February divided by 3. The monthly residential sewer bill remains30
level for 12 months until a new winter water use data match is completed. The winter period is relied
upon for a proxy measure of domestic water consumption as it is generally free of irrigation use. The
monthly commercial variable charge is $4.42 per 1,000 gallons of water use metered monthly. All
residential and commercial bills have a 5% surcharge which is a pass-through of a State gross receipts tax
imposed on proceeds from governmental utility services. Bills may be paid by mail, at City offices and
by direct bank debit.
Rate changes are usually made effective January 1 st. The last rate increase adopted for the sewer
system was effective January 1, 2018, which was the fourth of five planned 4.9% rate increases
implemented since 2016. The fifth and last such increase has been approved by the City Council and
became effective on January 1, 2019. No additional rate increases have been recommended or approved.
The following table sets forth the monthly wastewater fees charged to residential and commercial
customers within the City’s corporate limits in the current and the previous five fiscal years.

Sewer charges are a lienable assessment. The City annually files liens for assessments which are
in arrears. The lien filing process is pursued in accordance with State statute and City ordinance.
A sewer fee surcharge is charged to restaurants whose effluent constituents (i.e. grease) exceed
established wastewater parameters and which present specific treatment problems. The purpose of the
surcharge is to encourage the customer to institute measures to eliminate or reduce the problem
constituents. The City samples discharges and applies the surcharge based on the results of the tested
samples. Resampling may occur at the City’s discretion or at the discharger’s request, most notably after
corrective measures have been installed. Proceeds from the surcharge are considered part of the
Wastewater Utility System’s billed revenues.

Treated Effluent
Most of the treated effluent is released into the Santa Fe River. However, the City also allows
some of the treated effluent to be used for irrigation and construction applications. More extensive use of
effluent can either help reduce the demand for potable water or augment fresh water supply. For this
reason, interest in more extensive use of treated effluent has grown in proportion to the demand for water
generally in the area.
In 1998, a truck filling station for treated effluent was constructed on a site adjacent to the
Wastewater Treatment Facility. The City charges a fee of $3.03 per 1,000 gallons of treated effluent
purchased at this site. Proceeds from the charge collected at this site are included in Wastewater Utility
System Gross Revenues.
Effluent Use Contracts
The City has eight parties who regularly divert treated effluent for irrigation. The contractual
agreements allow the authorized user to divert effluent flow by means of pumps, meters and transmission
lines which have been installed and are maintained at the user’s expense. The City may suspend delivery
if required to do so by regulatory agencies.32
(a) Pursuant to an agreement, dated as of September 30, 1959, as amended and
supplemented by a supplemental agreement, dated as of June 25, 1964 (collectively, the “Country Club
Agreement”), between the City and the Santa Fe Country Club and Golf Association (the “Santa Fe
Country Club”), the Santa Fe Country Club diverts effluent for irrigation of an 18-hole golf course
located between the corporate limits of the City and the Wastewater Treatment Facility. The Santa Fe
Country Club is limited to a maximum withdrawal of 700,000 gallons per day. There is no monetary
compensation to the City for the use of the treated effluent. Rather, the Santa Fe Country Club allows
public use of the golf course subject to the payment of prevailing green fees. The Country Club
Agreement terminates upon the Country Club ceasing the operation of the golf course or the Wastewater
Treatment Facility is moved to another site. Over the last five calendar years, the Santa Fe Country Club
used an average of 108.78 million gallons of treated effluent per year.
(b) In 2003, the city entered into an agreement with Las Campanas, a private housing
development, for the delivery of treated effluent for irrigation of two (2) 18-hole golf courses located
north of the presumptive city limits. The City has agreed to make treated effluent available to Las
Campanas and is limited to a maximum withdraw of 1,000,000 gallons per day and not to exceed 450 acre
feet per year. Las Campanas is required to pay the City $3.03 per thousand gallons of treated effluent.
(c) In the late 1990’s, the City’s Parks and Recreation Department constructed a
pump station and a 5.5 mile water line to deliver effluent from the Wastewater Treatment Facility to a
golf course and team playing fields at the Municipal Recreation Complex (“MRC”). A neighboring
regional landfill constructed an extension to the effluent delivery system in order to use treated effluent
for dust control and revegetation. The regional landfill uses approximately 100,000 gallons of treated
effluent per day.
(d) In 2006, the City entered into an agreement with the USDA Forest Service
(Forest Service), an agency of the United States federal government, to deliver treated effluent for a
livestock water distribution system on the Caja del Rio range of the Santa Fe National Forest. Total
delivery under this agreement shall not exceed 15,000 gallons per day, and 100,000 gallons per month
and shall not exceed 1,200,000 gallons per years. In the event that the Forest Service exceeds the average
daily delivery, the Forest Service shall pay a $250 fine and $3.03 per thousand gallons for the over-
delivery.
(e) In 2006, the City entered into an agreement with the Pueblo of Pojoaque
Development Corporation (“PPDC”) for diversion of effluent for the irrigation and grooming of a race
track facility. The agreement with PPDC supersedes a treated effluent delivery agreement originally
entered into with Santa Fe Racing Inc. in October 1981. The agreement with PPDC was amended on
April 2011 and expired on November 2011 but the contract continues on a month to month basis until it is
renewed or unless otherwise terminated. Effluent use is limited to irrigation. The City has agreed to
make the treated effluent available to PPDC from March 1 through November 30 of each year. PPDC is
limited to a maximum withdrawal of treated effluent of 400,000 per day and 43.5 million gallons per year.
PPDC is required to pay the City $3.03 per thousand gallons of treated effluent for water that is used
outside of the infield. In exchange for the water used in the infield, the City is able to use the facility for
recreational activities. In the last five calendar years, treated effluent use at the property has averaged
40.81 million gallons per year.
(f) In 2016, the City entered into a contract with the Grand Prix de Santa Fe to
provide effluent to the equestrian racetrack. The contract provides for the delivery of effluent as
emergency backup water and terminates one year from the first delivery of effluent under the contract.
Demand by the Grand Prix cannot exceed 200,000 gallons on any day, 6,000,000 in any month, or33
15,000,000 gallons annually. The Grand Prix is required to pay the City $3.03 per thousand gallons of
treated effluent.
Treated Effluent Management Plan
In 1998, the City completed a long-term plan for the future management of effluent. The purpose
of the plan is to provide staff and the public with specific policies regarding alternative uses of effluent
into the future. The City is currently in the process of updating this plan. Among the various options and
combinations (including the attendant legal constraints) are:
(a) the continued sale or lease to private and public bodies;
(b) more direct use by the City to reduce potable water use; and
(c) various methods such as injection and other forms of groundwater recharge as
well as the piping of effluent directly to the Rio Grande which would allow additional diversion of
groundwater or water imported from the Rio Grande. In 2016, the City completed a reuse feasibility
study. In 2018, the City conducted a comprehensive evaluation of piping effluent to the Rio Grande. The
City is currently seeking federal funding for the effluent project with an estimated cost of $18,000,000 to
be completed over three years. The project is anticipated to make 2,500 acre/ft of water available.
The various options all involve further capital investment by the City. The scale and timing of
those capital improvements will relate to the policy choices made by the governing body.
Treatment Issues
The Wastewater Management Division has completed the most of the projects listed in the last
evaluation and plan. They are the installation of a new composting facility, two new secondary clarifiers,
a new the dissolved-air flotation unit, disc-filters, and a post aeration basin.
The City has developed a five-year Wastewater Treatment Facility capital improvement program
which addresses improvements to the capacity and operations as well as potentially more stringent future
regulatory requirements at the Wastewater Treatment Facility. The capital improvement program is in the
process of design and implementation. The Improvement Project is part of the capital improvement
program. As additional components of the capital improvement program, the City is in the design phase
of a primary electrical system upgrade and an aeration system upgrades which are scheduled to begin
construction in fiscal year 2020. As a part of the aeration system, the City will be installing new blowers
and adding a post aeration system to the return flows from the solids handling treatment process. The
facility is also working to upgrade the tertiary treatment and ultraviolet disinfection system.
The City continues to emphasize pretreatment of certain wastes as an alternative to further
modification of its operation. Despite not being heavily industrialized, the City’s treatment processes are
stressed by wastewater that has high organic content. Grease is a constituent which adversely affects both
the collection and the treatment system. Both of these have been problematic in a community with a large
number of dining, lodging and related establishments. For this reason the City has adopted specific
pretreatment parameters by ordinance, applies billing surcharges and emphasizes continued enforcement
through inspections and compliance testing. See “—User Rates and Billing” above.34
Collection Issues
The Wastewater Management Division also has a five-year Collection System Capital
Improvement Program, which consists of the replacement of old, deteriorated sewer lines with new lines.
The improvement program has allocated $2.0 million every other year to replace aged sewer lines.
Regulatory and Legal Matters
The City is under no State or EPA sanctions, such as administrative orders, nor is it in any
litigation concerning such matters with respect to the Wastewater Utility System. A few technical
violations of the NPDES permit have occurred in the last several years as a result of wastewater spills due
to localized line breaks or overflows brought on by localized blockages. There was one overflow that
occurred in fiscal year 2011. The City has an established emergency procedure for responding to such
occurrences and, after an event, evaluates causative factors and installs corrective measures. The
Wastewater Management Division received renewal of the National Pollutant Discharge Elimination
System (NPDES) permit September 1, 2016. This permit established effluent limitations for total nitrogen
and total phosphorus. The City requested and received a three (3) year compliance schedule to allow the
facility time to install instrumentation to monitor for these nutrients and other process performance
indicators, as well as time to conduct a study of the facility’s ability to remove nutrients through process
modifications and optimization. To date, half of the instrumentation has been installed and a nutrient
removal and treatment optimization study has been completed. The City is currently in compliance for
total phosphorus. Upgrades to the aeration system will complete the compliance with total nitrogen.
In 2005, the City adopted a modification of Section 9 (Industrial Pre-treatment Regulations and
Procedures) of the City’s Municipal Code regarding wastewater and sanitary sewers. Provisions include
local pretreatment parameters and procedures for the issuance of permits and other enforcement related to
prohibited or restricted discharges. The new language was intended to eliminate outdated provisions,
make procedures and standards more readily comprehensible and clarify due process in matters related to
discharge permits. The City has completed an evaluation of Technically Based Local Limits. Draft
modifications to Municipal Code related to the Technically Based Local Limits were submitted to and
approved by the United States Environmental Protection Agency. The City expects to receive an approval
letter within the next two months and will take forward the proposed changes to the City Council. There
is no significant impact anticipated for industrial users.35
